Marginal Critique of the Classification and Arguments
Marginal gloss critiques the classification and the rhetorical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criticisms leveled against the classification of four possibilities and against the rhetorical argument, as found in the marginal gloss.

Core Idea
The fourfold classification is not exhaustive, and the 'repulsion easier' argument is merely rhetorical.

After presenting the refutations, the text includes marginal comments (ḥāshiya) that critically examine both the classification and the arguments. This lesson delves into these critiques: the claim of four possibilities is challenged as not all-encompassing, and the 'repulsion easier' point is dismissed as rhetorical, not demonstrative. These annotations show how later scholars refined the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal critique of the classification
The gloss objects that the necessitating agent is not limited to the occurrence of an opposite, and the non-existent is not limited to the removal of a condition. Understand why this challenges the exhaustive claim.
This shows that the classification might miss cases like the removal of a prerequisite (not just a condition).
2
Read the critique of the rhetorical argument
The gloss states that the 'repulsion easier' argument is rhetorical (khiṭābī) and appeals to common intuition, but it is not logically binding. Learn why it is considered weak.
The gloss elaborates that the existing and occurring are equal in strength, and stability might even favor the existing.
3
Understand the overall scholarly refinement
Note that these critiques do not necessarily reject the refutation of the second possibility, but they question the way it is argued. This reflects the nuanced nature of kalam discourse.
This lesson connects to the broader tradition of critical commentary in Islamic theology.

Commentary
This passage classifies the rational possibilities for how an accident (عرض) may cease to exist and then refutes the first two of these possibilities. The classification states that the accident's cessation is either by itself (لذاته) or by another (بغيره). The 'other' is either an existent thing that necessitates non-existence essentially (a necessitating agent, like the occurrence of an opposite) or an existent thing that necessitates non-existence by choice (a volitional agent), or a non-existent thing (removal of a condition). These four possibilities cover all rational options. However, the author then declares them all invalid. The first refutation addresses cessation by itself: if the accident's essence necessitated its own non-existence, then it would never have existed in the first place, because what a thing's essence necessitates cannot be separated from it. The second refutation addresses cessation by the occurrence of an opposite. It argues that the occurrence of the opposite in the same locus is conditional upon the absence of the accident. If this absence were caused by the occurrence of the opposite, circularity arises: both the accident's absence and the opposite's occurrence depend on each other. Alternatively, using a rhetorical argument, since opposites are symmetrical, there is no reason to think the new opposite removes the existing one rather than the existing one repelling the new one. In fact, repulsion (دفع) is easier than removal (رفع) because removal requires the new opposite to occur and also remove the existing one, while repulsion only requires preventing the new opposite from occurring. The marginal glosses then critique the classification: the claim that there are only four possibilities is denied because the necessitating agent could be something other than an opposite (e.g., another type of accidental cause) and the non-existent could be something other than removal of a condition (e.g., an absence of a prerequisite). The marginalia also criticize the 'repulsion is easier' argument as merely rhetorical (خطابي), not demonstrative. A longer gloss elaborates that the argument is rhetorical and appeals to common intuitions, but logically the existing accident and the new opposite are equal in strength, and stability might favor the existing one; however, the whole discussion remains rhetorical.

Key Terms

دفع (repulsion)
رفع (removal)
حصر (limitation/exhaustion)

Misconception: One might think that the first refutation (self-cessation) is also subject to critique here, but the gloss only targets the classification and the rhetorical argument.
Correction: The gloss does not challenge the self-cessation refutation; it only questions the enumeration and the second refutation's rhetorical part.

Why does the marginal gloss deny that the classification is limited to four possibilities?

?

Have you ever assumed a list of possibilities was complete, only to later realize you missed an option? What does this gloss teach you about intellectual humility in your own reasoning about theological matters?
?
The gloss calls the 'repulsion easier' argument rhetorical—it appeals to common intuition. In your own life, where might you be relying on comfortable, intuitive ideas about God or existence instead of rigorous, evidence-based reasoning? Will you commit to examining one such intuition today?

The Condition for an Accident's Persistence
Explains the condition for an accident's persistence, avoiding infinite regress and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam thinkers explained the persistence of accidents without falling into infinite regress or circularity, using the concept of exchanged non-persistent accidents.

Core Idea
An accident persists through a series of substitute non-persistent accidents, like frames in a film.

In this passage, al-Iji (or the commentator) addresses a delicate theological problem: how can an accident like color continue to exist if accidents are renewed moment by moment? The solution lies in a clever distinction between persistent and non-persistent accidents, and the idea that a chain of non-persistent accidents can serve as the condition for a persistent one, without leading to infinite regress or circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a very compressed style.
2
Identify the two types of accidents
The passage presents a classification of accidents into two types: those that may persist (like colors) and those that do not persist (like movements). Identify these and their characteristics.
3
Understand the main solution
The condition for a persistent accident is a series of non-persistent accidents that replace each other. Grasp how this avoids infinite regress.
Focus on the example of multiple rotations of movement.
4
Consider the alternative formulation
The passage also offers an alternative involving substance to avoid circularity. Understand how this works.
The condition of the persistent accident is substance, and condition of substance is the series of non-persistent accidents as a type.
5
Reflect on the necessity of exchange
Why must the condition involve a series of non-persistent accidents? Because a single non-persistent accident cannot sustain anything.
This ties back to the nature of non-persistent accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الشرط أعراضاً لا تبقى على التبادل إلى أن تنتهي إلى ما لا بدل عنه وعنده أنَّ الأعراض عندنا قسمان: قسم يجوز بقاؤه كالألوان وقسم لا يجوز بقاؤه كالحركات وحينئذ جاز أن يقال: شرط العرض الباقي عرض لا بعينه من الأعراض التي لا تبقى بذاتها كدورات متعددة من الحركات مثلا فيكون كل واحد من تلك الأعراض المتعددة بدلاً عن الآخر فيستمر وجود ذلك العرض باستمرار شرطه ما دام يتبادل تلك الأعراض فإذا انتهت إلى ما لا بدل عنه كالدورة الأخيرة من تلك الدورات المعدودة فقد زال الشرط فيزول العرض الباقي بلا تسلسل وجاز أيضاً أن يقال: شرط العرض الباقي هو الجوهر وشرط الجوهر هو تلك الأعراض المتبادلة فلا يلزم دور وإنما اعتبر في الشرط تبادل الأعراض غير القارة لأن الواحد من هذه الأعراض لا بقاء له فلا يبقى ما هو مشروط به هكذا ينبغي أن يضبط هذا الكلام
That condition: accidents that do not persist in exchange until they end at what has no substitute. And according to him: accidents with us are of two types: a type whose persistence is permissible like colors, and a type whose persistence is not permissible like movements. Then it is permissible to say: the condition of the remaining accident is an accident not identical to any of the accidents that do not persist by themselves, like multiple rotations of movements for example, so each one of those multiple accidents becomes a substitute for the other, so the existence of that accident continues by the continuation of its condition as long as those accidents are exchanged. When they end at what has no substitute, like the last cycle of those counted rotations, then the condition ceases, so the remaining accident ceases without infinite regress. And it is also permissible to say: the condition of the remaining accident is the substance, and the condition of the substance is those exchanged accidents, so no circularity occurs. Indeed, the exchange of non-enduring accidents is considered in the condition because the individual of these accidents has no persistence, so what is conditioned by it does not persist. Thus should this speech be regulated.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for the persistence of an accident. It begins by classifying accidents into two types: those that may persist (like colors) and those that do not persist (like movements). The condition for a persistent accident is a non-persistent accident that is replaced by another of the same kind, similar to multiple rotations of movements. Each successive accident substitutes for the previous one, allowing the persistent accident to continue as long as the exchange continues. When the series ends at a final accident that has no substitute, the condition is removed and the persistent accident ceases, avoiding infinite regress. An alternative formulation is that the condition of the persistent accident is the substance, and the condition of the substance is those exchanged accidents, which avoids circularity because the substance's persistence is conditioned by the series of accidents as a type, not by any particular accident. The exchange of non-enduring accidents is necessary because a single non-enduring accident cannot sustain anything.

عرض باقٍ
عرض غير باقٍ
شرط البقاء
تبادل الأعراض
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the condition of the accident is a single specific accident.
Correction: The condition is actually a series of non-persistent accidents that replace each other, not a single accident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the persistence of an accident like a color?
A) A single permanent accident
B) A series of exchanged non-persistent accidents
C) The substance alone
D) The accident itself

Key Takeaway
A persistent accident is sustained by a series of non-persistent accidents that replace each other, ending without infinite regress; an alternative formulation using the substance avoids circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, a persistent accident is sustained by a series of non-persistent accidents that replace each other. Consider your own faith (iman). Is your faith sustained by a series of transient acts of obedience that constantly renew it? If the series stops, does your faith begin to fade? Examine your daily routine: Are you providing a continuous chain of good deeds, or are you relying on a single past deed to sustain your faith? What does your current condition say about the persistence of your faith?
?
The passage warns that a single non-persistent accident cannot sustain another accident because it has no persistence itself. Reflect on your own good deeds: Can a single act of charity or prayer from last year sustain your spiritual state indefinitely? Or do you need a fresh, continuous stream of acts to maintain your connection with Allah? Where are you relying on past deeds to carry you forward without renewal?
?
The alternative solution avoids circularity by making the substance the condition conditioned by the series. In your life, what is the 'substance' that sustains you? Is it your soul, your intention, your relationship with Allah? And what is the series that conditions it? Are you maintaining that series, or is it broken? Ask yourself: If the series of my prayers, fasting, and charity were to end, would my faith immediately cease? Am I taking my faith for granted, assuming it will persist without constant renewal?

The Observation Argument for Accident Endurance
First proof for accident endurance by observation and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why observation of apparent endurance does not prove that accidents are actually enduring, and how the refutation uses the concept of 'successive similars' to explain sensory continuity.

Core Idea
Seeing is not believing – sensory continuity can be an illusion of rapid replacement.

The first argument for the endurance of accidents appeals to direct observation: we see colors persisting. But is seeing always reliable? Sanusi's refutation reveals a key philosophical insight: what appears continuous may actually be a rapid succession of discrete entities. This lesson examines the observation argument and its critical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first proof: observation of colors enduring. Notice how the refutation is introduced with 'قلنا'.
The passage includes a commentary line about sense error at the end.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that observation does not prove a single continuous entity; it may be a rapid succession of similar accidents. Use the water pipe analogy to grasp this: discrete drops appear as a continuous stream.
This is the key concept of 'tawarud al-amthal' (succession of similars).
3
Consider the additional point
The commentary adds that even if observation were reliable, the senses often err. This reinforces the weakness of the proof.
Not all sensory illusions are obvious.

Then for those who affirm the endurance of accidents there are methods. The first: observation, for we see colors enduring; denying their endurance is an attack on necessary truths. (We say: there is no indication for it, i.e., for observation, that what is observed is one continuous thing, because it is possible that they are successive similars without separation, like water gushing from a pipe, seen as one continuous thing, but in reality they are similars succeeding continuously. The second: that it is said: If you allow the succession of similars in accidents, then let its like be allowed in bodies, resulting in the inability to be certain of the endurance of bodies, which is invalid by consensus. (We say: what you mentioned is an analogy and juridical reasoning without a common factor, so it is invalid. And our judgment of the endurance of bodies is not by observation of their continuity, so that the observation of continuity could be made a common factor in that analogy. Rather, our judgment of the endurance of bodies is by rational necessity, not by sensory observation, and because if not, i.e., if bodies did not endure, death and life would be inconceivable.
His statement: (there is no indication for it, etc.) Even if granted, the senses often err, so let this be among their errors.
His statement: (the second, etc.) Its summary is an analogical argument for the endurance of accidents based on the endurance of bodies, with the common factor being the observation of their continuity, and the doubt cast on the endurance of accidents due to the possibility of their continuity being by renewal of similars necessitates doubt in the endurance of bodies accordingly.
His statement: (by rational necessity) For reason judges intuitively that if bodies did not endure, security in reason, custom, and law would be lost, and order would be disrupted.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the endurance of accidents and their refutations. The first proof is from observation: we see colors lasting, so they must endure. The refutation is that observation does not guarantee a single continuous entity; it could be a rapid succession of similar accidents, like water from a pipe appearing continuous but actually being discrete drops. The second proof is an analogy: if we allow successive similars for accidents, then the same could be said for bodies, undermining our certainty of body endurance. The refutation is that this is a legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi) lacking a relevant common factor between accidents and bodies; moreover, body endurance is known by rational necessity, not by observation, so the analogy fails. The commentary adds that sensory observation often errs, and that rational necessity (e.g., the impossibility of conceiving life and death without enduring bodies) grounds our certainty about bodies. Thus, both proofs for accident endurance are invalid.

Key Terms
مشاهدة (mushahada)
توارد الأمثال (tawarud al-amthal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of the observation argument implies that nothing can be known through the senses.
Correction: The refutation only shows that sensory continuity is not proof of substantial permanence; it does not deny all sensory knowledge. The senses can be reliable for other purposes, but in this specific claim, they fall short.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does observation not prove the endurance of accidents?
A) Because colors change over time.
B) Because the observed continuity could be due to a rapid succession of similar accidents.
C) Because the senses are always wrong.
D) Because accidents are not observable at all.

Key Takeaway
Observation of apparent continuity does not prove actual endurance because it could be a rapid succession of similar accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the continuity you see with your eyes could be an illusion of rapid replacement, what does that say about your reliance on your own perception in matters of faith? Is your certainty in Allah based on what you see, or on what you know through revelation?
?
Consider a habit or belief you hold because 'it has always been that way' in your experience. How might the concept of tawarud al-amthal challenge you to examine whether that continuity is truly enduring or merely a repeated accident from Allah?
?
When you look at yourself and see a continuous self across years, do you recognize that each moment is a fresh creation by Allah, or do you fall into the illusion of self-subsistence? How can this realization deepen your gratitude and reliance on Him every second?

The Analogy Argument for Accident Endurance and Its Refutation
Second proof for accident endurance by analogy with bodies and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for accident endurance based on analogy with bodies, and why the refutation rejects it as a flawed juridical analogy lacking a proper common factor.

Core Idea
Analogies require a relevant common factor; body endurance is known by reason, not by observation.

The second argument for the endurance of accidents employs an analogy: if successive similars are allowed for accidents, then the same reasoning would undermine our certainty about bodies. Since we are certain bodies endure, accidents must also endure. Sanusi's refutation dismantles this analogy by pointing out that body endurance is known by rational necessity, not by observation, so the analogy lacks a valid common factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof and refutation
Read the passage for the second proof. Notice the structure: the opponent proposes an analogy (qiyas) between accidents and bodies. The refutation responds with 'قلنا'.
The passage also includes commentary explaining the analogy.
2
Understand the refutation's key point
The refutation rejects the analogy as a 'juridical analogy' (qiyas fiqhi) without a relevant common factor (jami). The common factor would need to be the observation of continuity, but body endurance is not known by observation.
Rational necessity (darura aqliyya) grounds our knowledge of body endurance.
3
Consider the commentary
The commentary adds that without body endurance, life and death would be inconceivable, and social order would collapse. This reinforces that body endurance is a rational necessity.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic theology.

The Argument from Observation for Accident Endurance and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the argument from observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from observation for the endurance of accidents is flawed due to the possibility of succession of similars.

Core Idea
Observation alone cannot prove endurance; what is seen may be rapid succession.

We now examine the first argument used by proponents of accident endurance: the argument from observation. Al-Jurjani presents the claim that since we observe colors persisting, their endurance is self-evident. He then refutes it by showing that sensory observation is insufficient to distinguish between true endurance and rapid replacement of like accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Identify the argument and refutation

3
Understand the concept of 'succession of similars'
Reflect on how the water example illustrates that continuity in perception does not guarantee real continuity. Consider why this undermines the argument from observation.
This is a key kalam concept.

قوله : (بالضرورة (العقلية فإنَّ العقل يحكم بديهة بأنه لولا بقاء الأجسام لارتفع الأمان عن العقل والعرف والشرع واختل النظام.
يمكن أن يكون زوال العرض العرض يخلقه الله تعالى لا في محل وأما كون زوال العرض لعرض يخلقه الله تعالى في محل العرض الزائل فيزول في ثاني الحال، فيزول العرض القائم به فهو راجع إلى اشتراط بقاء الجوهر في بقاء العرض وقد مر ما فيه تأمل .
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين
لأن الموت كما هو المشهور عدم الحياة عن محل اتصف بها وإذا لم تكن الأجسام باقية كان محل الموت غير الجسم الموصوف بالحياة
Then for those who affirm the endurance of accidents there are methods. The first: observation. For we observe colors enduring; denying their endurance is an attack on necessities. We say: it (observation) has no indication that the observed is one continuous thing, because it is possible that they are similars succeeding without interval—like water gushing from a pipe, seen as one continuous thing by observation, while in reality it is similars succeeding in continuity. The second: that it is said: if you allow the succession of similars in accidents, then let the like be allowed in bodies, so that one would not be certain of the endurance of bodies, which is false by agreement. We say: what you mentioned is a likeness and legal analogy without a commonality, so it is invalid. And our judgment of the endurance of bodies is not by observation of their continuity so that observation of continuity could be made a common cause in that likeness; rather, our judgment of the endurance of bodies is by rational necessity, not by sensory observation. And because if it were not for that—i.e., if not for the endurance of bodies—death and life would not be conceivable. He said: (i.e., by this proof) which indicates that endurance entails impossibility of passing away, by affirming the same premise to produce the same result, as indicated by his saying 'since we have shown that endurance entails impossibility'; so it is not objected that the previous proof used exclusion of the opposite of the consequent to produce the opposite of the premise—how then can it be used to argue that the world does not perish? He said: (and the annihilation of bodies is not valid, etc.) He indicated by the conjunction that the meaning is not that the world in all its parts, substances and accidents, does not perish, for denying the passing away of accidents is obstinacy; rather the meaning is that bodies do not perish, but change occurs in them from state to state and from composition to analysis. He said: (in the discussions, etc.) i.e., in the notice mentioned in the third objective. He said: (no indication for it, etc.) and even if it be granted, the senses err often, so let this be one of their errors. He said: (the second, etc.) its summary is: a comparison of the endurance of accidents to the endurance of bodies by the commonality of the observation of their continuity; and doubt about the endurance of accidents because their continuity could be due to renewal of similars necessitates doubt about the endurance of bodies for that reason. He said: (by rational necessity) for the intellect judges intuitively that were it not for the endurance of bodies, security would be removed from intellect, custom, and law, and order would be disturbed. It is possible that the passing away of an accident is by an accident that God creates in no locus; and as for the passing away of an accident by an accident that God creates in the locus of the passing accident, it passes away in the second moment, and then the accident subsisting in it passes away—that goes back to the condition of the substance's endurance for the endurance of the accident, and there has passed what is to be considered in it. The first lookout - the seventh objective: that the accident does not endure for two moments. Because death, as is well-known, is the absence of life from a locus that was qualified by it; and if bodies were not enduring, the locus of death would be other than the body qualified by life.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments for the endurance of accidents (الأعراض) and their refutations, along with extensive commentary. The first argument is from observation: we see colors remaining, so denying their endurance contradicts self-evident truths. The refutation explains that sensory observation does not guarantee that what is seen is a single continuous entity; it could be a succession of similar accidents replacing each other without interval, like water flowing from a pipe, which appears continuous but is actually discrete particles. The second argument is an analogy: if you allow the succession of similars in accidents, then you should also allow it in bodies, which would undermine certainty about bodily endurance—an unacceptable conclusion. The refutation states that this is a legal analogy without a common cause (جامع): the certainty of bodily endurance is not based on sensory observation of continuity but on rational necessity (الضرورة العقلية). The commentator explains each part: the first gloss clarifies that the earlier proof was not circular; the second gloss distinguishes that the claim is not that all things including accidents are imperishable, but that bodies specifically do not perish; the third gloss refers to a previous discussion; the fourth gloss notes that even if observation were accepted, the senses can err; the fifth gloss summarizes the second argument; the sixth gloss expands on rational necessity—without bodily endurance, trust in intellect, custom, and religion would collapse. The final section extends the second argument's refutation by discussing death: if bodies did not endure, the locus of death would be different from the living body, which is absurd. Thus both arguments fail to prove the endurance of accidents.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
بقاء (endurance)
توارد الأمثال (succession of similars)
مشاهدة (observation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That observing a color remaining over time proves the accident endures.
Correction: Observation cannot distinguish between true endurance and a rapid succession of like accidents; therefore, it is not a valid proof.

Checkpoint
What example does Al-Jurjani use to illustrate that observation may show continuity without real endurance?
A) A burning candle
B) Water gushing from a pipe
C) A falling leaf
D) The rising sun

Key Takeaway
Sensory observation is insufficient to prove the endurance of accidents because apparent continuity could be due to succession of similars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own faith and practice, what 'continuous stream' do you assume is solid and enduring, but might actually be a succession of moments that only remain if Allah constantly renews them? What one habit will you start today to consciously treat each moment as a gift, not a given?
?
The water pipe analogy shows that what seems one thing can be many fleeting pieces. In your relationship with Allah, where have you relied on 'observation'—what you can see or feel—to judge its reality, rather than on the unseen truth that He is always near? What will you change so that your certainty rests on revealed knowledge, not on sensory comfort?

The Argument from Analogy for Accident Endurance and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the analogical argument for accident endurance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogical argument from bodies fails to prove accident endurance and the role of rational necessity in affirming bodily endurance.

Core Idea
Bodily endurance is known by rational necessity, not observation; thus analogy with bodies is invalid.

The second argument attempts to draw an analogy between accidents and bodies: if you allow succession in accidents, you must allow it in bodies, which would undermine certainty of bodily endurance. Al-Jurjani rejects this as a faulty legal analogy lacking a common cause, explaining that bodily endurance is known by rational necessity, not observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the analogical argument and its refutation.
Notice the shift from observation to rational necessity.
2
Analyze the structure of the refutation
Al-Jurjani points out that the analogy is a legal analogy without a common ground (جامع). He then states that the judgment of bodily endurance is based on rational necessity, not observation. Identify these two parts.
3
Reflect on rational necessity
Consider why rational necessity is a stronger basis than observation. Think about the consequences if bodies did not endure—order would collapse.
The concept of rational necessity is central to kalam epistemology.

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments for the endurance of accidents (الأعراض) and their refutations, along with extensive commentary. The first argument is from observation: we see colors remaining, so denying their endurance contradicts self-evident truths. The refutation explains that sensory observation does not guarantee that what is seen is a single continuous entity; it could be a succession of similar accidents replacing each other without interval, like water flowing from a pipe, which appears continuous but is actually discrete particles. The second argument is an analogy: if you allow the succession of similars in accidents, then you should also allow it in bodies, which would undermine certainty about bodily endurance—an unacceptable conclusion. The refutation states that this is a legal analogy without a common cause (جامع): the certainty of bodily endurance is not based on sensory observation of continuity but on rational necessity (الضرورة العقلية). The commentator explains each part: the first gloss clarifies that the earlier proof was not circular; the second gloss distinguishes that the claim is not that all things including accidents are imperishable, but that bodies specifically do not perish; the third gloss refers to a previous discussion; the fourth gloss notes that even if observation were accepted, the senses can err; the fifth gloss summarizes the second argument; the sixth gloss expands on rational necessity—without bodily endurance, trust in intellect, custom, and religion would collapse. The final section extends the second argument's refutation by discussing death: if bodies did not endure, the locus of death would be different from the living body, which is absurd. Thus both arguments fail to prove the endurance of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might this affect your gratitude to the One who sustains that necessity?
?
If the endurance of bodies is a rational necessity, what does that imply about the certainty of the after life? The very fact that you can conceive of life and death presupposes a stable body. How does this rational baseline challenge you to treat your body as an amanah (trust) from Allah, with accountability?
?
The opponent's analogy fails because it borrows a basis (observation) from one domain to another. In your own thinking, do you ever analogize divine attributes to human attributes without considering the different epistemic ground? What would al-Jurjani's refutation say to that, and how can you re-train your mind to avoid such an analogy in your faith?

The Necessary Judgment: One Accident Cannot Subsist in Two Loci
Sanusi asserts the necessary judgment and clarifies via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of one accident existing in two loci is a necessary judgment, known by intuition rather than inference.

Core Idea
The impossibility of one accident in two loci is as self-evident as a body in two places.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes a foundational principle of kalam: that an individual accident cannot simultaneously exist in two different loci. He argues that this judgment is not derived from reasoning but is known directly by necessity. By drawing an analogy to the impossibility of a single body occupying two places, he clarifies that both judgments are equally self-evident, while also noting a key distinction between the two relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words convey the meaning.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic; they may be OCR artifacts but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the main claim and its justification
Find the statement that the accident does not subsist in two loci and note that it is described as necessary (ضرورة). Understand that this means it is self-evident, not inferred.
3
Examine the analogy with the body
Read the comparison: just as a body cannot be in two places at once, one accident cannot be in two loci. The author says both judgments are equally intuitive.
4
Note the distinction between the two relations
The author adds that the accident-locus relation is not exactly like the body-place relation, because multiple accidents can coexist in one locus, while two bodies cannot occupy the same place. This prevents a potential objection.
This shows careful reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : العرض لا يقوم بمحلين (العرض) الواحد بالشخص ) لا يقوم بمحلين ضرورة ) أي هذا حكم معلوم بالضرورة ولذلك نجزم بأن السواد القائم بهذا المحل غير السواد ( القائم بالمحل الآخر) جزماً يقينياً لا نحتاج فيه إلى فكر ) ولا فرق بينه ) أي بين جزمنا بأن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بمحلين ( وبين جزمنا بأن الجسم الواحد ( لا يوجد ) في آن واحد ( في مكانين فكما أنَّ الجزم الثاني بديهي بلا شبهة فكذا الأول ولسنا نقول نسبة العرض إلى المحل كنسبة الجسم إلى المكان فلو جاز حلوله في محلين لجاز حصول الجسم في مكانين حتى يرد عليه أنَّ النسبتين ليستا على السواء لا مكان حلول أعراض متعددة معاً في محل واحد وامتناع اجتماع جسمين في مكان واحد
The eighth goal: The accident does not subsist in two loci. The one individual accident does not subsist in two loci necessarily, i.e., this judgment is known by necessity. Therefore we judge with certainty, without need for reflection, that the blackness subsisting in this locus is not the blackness subsisting in that other locus. And there is no difference between our judgment that one accident does not subsist in two loci and our judgment that one body does not exist in two places at one time. Just as the second judgment is self-evidently clear, so is the first. And we do not say that the relation of accident to locus is like the relation of body to place; for if it were permissible for it to subsist in two loci, it would be permissible for a body to occupy two places. This would be rejected because the two relations are not equivalent, as multiple accidents can coexist in one locus while two bodies cannot coexist in one place.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines the impossibility of one accident subsisting in two loci as a necessary judgment. The author clarifies that this is known by intuition, not by inference, using the example of blackness in two loci. He draws an analogy to the impossibility of one body being in two places simultaneously, emphasizing that both judgments are self-evident. He then distinguishes the accident-locus relation from the body-place relation, noting that multiple accidents can coexist in one locus, whereas two bodies cannot occupy the same place. The purpose is to prevent a potential objection that the analogy is imperfect. This is a definition and clarification.

محل
ضرورة
بديهي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of one accident in two loci is inferred from the analogy with the body.
Correction: The author explicitly states that both judgments are necessary and self-evident, not inferred. The analogy is used for clarification, not as a proof.

Checkpoint
What is the status of the judgment that one accident cannot subsist in two loci according to the author?
A) Inferred by reason
B) Known by necessity
C) Based on scripture
D) Derived from analogy

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of one accident in two loci is a necessary, self-evident judgment, not derived from analogy or inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at two objects of the same color, do you instinctively know that the color in one is not the same individual color in the other? Why is this certainty so immediate? Now consider your certainty about Allah's existence—is it equally immediate, or do you rely on proofs? What does your answer reveal about your faith?
?
This lesson describes a judgment so clear that it requires no thought. In your life, have you ever rejected a truth that is actually self-evident because you overcomplicated it? What can you do today to trust necessary knowledge about Allah and His creation more than your own reasoning?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between the relation of accident to locus and body to place. How does this distinction prevent confusion in your own understanding of Allah's attributes? Are you careful not to compare Allah's attributes to created things in a way that leads to error? Commit to memorizing one analogy from this lesson to guard against that mistake.

Objection and Answer on Composition Not Subsisting in Three Parts
Sanusi presents an objection and answer about composition when one of three parts is removed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves an apparent contradiction about composition by distinguishing between two numerically distinct accidents that share the same essential nature.

Core Idea
Two accidents can be identical in essence but distinct in individual identity.

This passage tackles a subtle objection to the rule that an accident cannot subsist in two loci. The objector claims that composition among three parts would be destroyed if one part is removed, yet the two remaining parts still exhibit composition. Sanusi's answer hinges on a crucial distinction: the composition between the two is a different accident from the composition among the three, even though they share the same reality. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of objection and answer.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the objection's logic
Find the premise: absence of one part destroys composition. Then the objector claims the consequent is false because the two parts still have composition. Understand why this seems contradictory.
The objection relies on the principle that an accident cannot subsist in two loci.
3
Analyze the answer's distinction
Sanusi distinguishes two compositions: one between the two parts, and another among the three. They are different individuals (شخص) but same specific reality (حقيقة نوعية).
The negation applies only to the composition among the three.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this distinction preserves the rule that accidents do not subsist in multiple loci while explaining the evident composition between remaining parts.
This is a classic kalam move: individuation by subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : العرض لا يقوم بمحلين مثلاً لعدم التأليف بعدم جزء واحد من تلك ( الثلاثة ( لأن عدم المحل يستلزم عدم الحال فيه ) والتالي باطل لأنَّ الجزأين الباقيين بينهما تأليف قطعاً ) لأن صعوبة الانفكاك باقية بينهما ( وجوابه أنَّ التأليف الذي بين الجزأين غير التأليف ( الذي بين الثلاثة أي يجوز أن يقوم تأليف واحد الجزأين كما ذكرته ويقوم تأليف آخر بثلاثة أجزاء فيكون هذا التأليف القائم بالثلاثة مغايراً بالشخص للتأليف الأول القائم بالجزأين ( وإن ماثله ) في الحقيقة النوعية ( والمنفي ( عند ما عدم واحد من الثلاثة ( هو ) التأليف ( الثاني ) القائم بالثلاثة دون التأليف الأول القائم بالاثنين فلا يلزم حينئذ انعدام التأليف بينهما
The First Observatory - The Eighth Objective: The accident does not subsist in two loci. For example, the composition is missing due to the absence of one part of those three, because the absence of the locus necessitates the absence of the accident in it. The consequent is false because between the two remaining parts there is definitely composition, because the difficulty of separation remains between them. The answer is that the composition between the two parts is not the composition between the three; i.e., it is possible that one composition subsists in the two parts as you mentioned, and another composition subsists in three parts, so this composition subsisting in the three is different in individual identity from the first composition subsisting in the two parts, even if it resembles it in specific reality. What is negated when one of the three is absent is the second composition subsisting in the three, not the first composition subsisting in the two; so then the absence of composition between them does not follow.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the claim that an accident (عرض) cannot subsist in two loci. The specific case is composition (تأليف) among three parts. The objector argues: if one of the three parts is absent, the composition is lost, because the absence of the locus necessitates the absence of the accident. But the consequent is false, because between the two remaining parts there is definitely composition, since they are still connected. The answer is that there are two distinct compositions: one composition subsists in the two parts (the original pair), and another composition subsists in all three parts. When one part is removed, the second composition (the one involving the third part) is negated, but the first composition (between the two parts) remains. Thus, no contradiction arises.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
تأليف
شخص
حقيقة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the same composition accident exists in both two and three parts.
Correction: The answer clarifies that they are distinct individuals (شخص) even if they share the same specific reality.

Checkpoint
Why does the absence of one of three parts not necessarily destroy composition between the remaining two?
A) Because composition is an accident that cannot be destroyed.
B) Because the composition between the two parts is a different individual from the composition among the three.
C) Because the two parts are still connected by a third part.
D) Because composition does not depend on the number of parts.

Key Takeaway
Accidents can be specifically identical but numerically distinct due to their subjects, preserving the rule that no accident subsists in two loci.

Three Cases of an Accident Subsisting in a Divisible Locus
Sanusi classifies three possibilities for a single accident subsisting in a divisible locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible cases for a single accident subsisting in a divisible locus and which are accepted, disputed, or self-evidently false.

Core Idea
A single accident can divide with its locus, remain undivided, or exist in two places—only the last is impossible.

Sanusi introduces a classification that is fundamental to understanding how accidents relate to loci in kalām. He distinguishes between cases that are universally accepted, disputed, and self-evidently false. This classification will later help resolve disputes about the nature of composition, such as the view attributed to Abu Hashim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three cases
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the translation. As you read, note the three distinct scenarios Sanusi presents for a single accident (عرض واحد بالشخص) subsisting in a divisible locus (محل منقسم).
The first case is introduced with 'يجوز' (permissible), the second with 'مختلف فيه' (disputed), and the third with 'بطلانه بديهي' (self-evidently false).
2
Understand the status of each case
For each of the three cases, determine whether Sanusi declares it undisputed, disputed, or self-evidently false. Reflect on why the third case is impossible: the same accident cannot exist in two separate loci at once.
The third case's impossibility is considered intuitive (بديهي).
3
Apply the classification to Abu Hashim's view
Read the final part of the passage where Sanusi discusses what is reported from Abu Hashim about 'التأليف' (composition). Consider how the three cases help interpret which sense Abu Hashim intended, and why the well-known interpretation (third case) leads to rejection.
Sanusi shows that the apparent dispute over whether composition is existent may be resolved by clarifying which case is meant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن العرض الواحد بالشخص يجوز قيامه بمحل منقسم بحيث ينقسم ذلك العرض بانقسامه حتى يوجد كل جزء منه في جزء من محله فهذا مما لا نزاع فيه وقيامه بمحل منقسم على وجه لا ينقسم بانقسام محله مختلف فيه كما سيأتي وأما قيامه بمحل مع قيامه بعينه بمحل آخر فهو الذي ذكرنا أنَّ بطلانه بديهي وما نقل عن أبي هاشم في التأليف إن حمل على القسم الأول فلا منازعة معه إلافي انقسام التأليف، وكونه وجودياً وإن حمل على القسم الثاني فبعد تسليم جوازه تبقى المناقشة في وجودية التأليف والمشهور أنَّ مراده القسم الثالث الذي علم بطلانه بديهة .
Know that a single individual accident may subsist in a divisible locus such that the accident divides by its division, so that each part of it exists in a part of its locus—this is undisputed. Its subsistence in a divisible locus in a way that it does not divide by the division of its locus is disputed, as will come. As for its subsistence in one locus while the very same accident subsists in another locus—this is what we mentioned: its falsity is self-evident. What is reported from Abu Hashim regarding composition, if interpreted as the first case, then there is no dispute with him except regarding the divisibility of composition and its being existent. If interpreted as the second case, then after conceding its possibility, the debate remains about the existence of composition. The well-known view is that he intended the third case, whose falsity is known intuitively.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage classifies three cases of an accident (عرض) subsisting in a locus (محل). First case: the accident is divisible and each part of it subsists in a part of a divisible locus—this is accepted without dispute. Second case: a single accident subsists in a divisible locus without dividing—this is disputed. Third case: a single accident subsists in one locus and the very same accident subsists in another locus—this is self-evidently false. The passage then applies this to the reported view of Abu Hashim on composition: if his claim is about the first case, the only issue is whether composition is divisible and existent; if about the second, the debate is about its existence; but the well-known interpretation is that he meant the third case, which is obviously false.

Key Terms
عرض واحد بالشخص
محل منقسم
انقسام
بديهي
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that any single accident cannot subsist in multiple loci at all, but the first two cases show exceptions.
Correction: The first case shows that a single accident can be spread across a divisible locus if the accident itself divides, and the second case allows a single accident to be present in a divisible locus without dividing. Only the simultaneous existence of the same accident in two separate loci is impossible.

Checkpoint
Which case does Sanusi describe as self-evidently false?
A) An accident subsisting in a divisible locus while dividing with it.
B) An accident subsisting in a divisible locus without dividing.
C) The same accident subsisting in two different loci.
D) An accident subsisting only in one locus.

Key Takeaway
The self-evident falsity of a single accident subsisting in two separate loci is a foundational principle in kalām, used to reject certain views like that attributed to Abu Hashim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you held onto a belief that, deep down, you knew was impossible—like the self-evident falsity Sanusi describes? What kept you from admitting it?
?
This passage divides truths into undisputed, disputed, and self-evidently false. In your own spiritual life, is there a self-evident truth (e.g., the need for prayer) that you are treating as disputed or ignoring? What would it cost you to embrace it fully?
?
Sanusi rejects Abu Hashim's view by correctly classifying it. Is there a sin or habit you have misclassified in your mind—calling it 'permissible' when it is clearly forbidden—because you want to keep it? Will you now call it by its true name?

The Second Proof: Unity and Division in a Subject
Al-Jurjani presents the second proof, objection, and response regarding unity and division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument against the external existence of unity, the objection based on non-saryani indwelling, and the author's response.

Core Idea
Unity cannot inhere in a divisible subject without dividing itself.

Al-Jurjani presents a second argument: if unity exists as an accident in a body, it would have to be either wholly in one part, not in any part, or distributed — all impossible. The philosophers object by proposing that an accident can inhere in a subject as a whole without penetrating its parts. This lesson unpacks the argument and the subsequent debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the tripartite division of possibilities in the proof.
2
Identify the three cases in the proof
The proof examines three scenarios for how unity (an accident) might inhere in a divisible subject. Find each one and understand why each is impossible.
These correspond to the three 'if' clauses in the text.
3
Understand the objection: non-saryani indwelling
The philosophers introduce a fourth possibility: the accident inheres in the whole subject as a totality, not in its parts. Grasp how this challenges the proof.
This is the 'حلول غير سرياني'.
4
Examine the response and the commentator's note
The author argues that even in non-saryani indwelling, each part is characterized by a part of the accident. Note the commentator's remark that there is discussion about this.
The response attempts to show that division still occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني إِنَّ الواحد قد يقبل القسمة كالجسم الواحد ( وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام ما حل فيه لأنه إن كان الحال الذي هو الوحدة مثلاً ( في جزء منه كان ( ذلك الجزء من المحل ( هو الواحد ) لأن الوحدة قائمة به ( دون الكل ) والمقدر خلافه ( وإن لم يكن الحال ( في شيء من أجزائه لم يكن بالضرورة صفة (له أتي للمحل الذي فرضناه موصوفاً به وهذا أيضاً باطل ( وإن كان ) ( الحال ( في كل جزء من المحل ( فإما بالتمام فيقوم الواحد ) الشخصي (بالكثير) وقد عرفت بطلانه بديهة ) أو لا بالتمام فيكون جزء منه قائما بجزء وجزء بآخر وهو المراد بالانقسام يعني انقسام الحال بحسب انقسام المحل وقد اعترض على هذا الاستدلال بأنه يجوز أن يقوم الحال بمجموع المحل المنقسم من حيث هو مجموع، ويكون صفة له وإن لم ينقسم بانقسامه. ومثل ذلك يسمى حلولاً غير سرياني فأشار إليه وإلى جوابه بقوله : ( وقول من قال : (هذا) الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يصح فيما يكون الحلول) في المحل المنقسم ( حلول السريان فيه إذ بدونه لا يلزم انقسام الحال بانقسام محله ) ولا طائل (له ( أي لا فائدة فيه ( لأنا برهنا على أن كل جزء من المحل ) المنقسم الذي حل فيه صفة ) متصف بجزء منها ولا معنى للسريان إلا ذلك ) وفيه بحث
The second: indeed, the one accepts division, like one body; and the division of the subject necessitates the division of what inheres in it. For if the inherence, which is for example the unity, is in a part of it, then that part of the subject is the one, because the unity subsists in it, not in the whole — and the assumed is opposite. And if the inherence is not in any of its parts, it is not necessarily a predicate for it, so the subject we assumed would not be described by it, and this also is false. And if the inherence is in every part of the subject, then either wholly — so the individual one subsists in many, and you have known its falsehood intuitively — or not wholly, so part of it subsists in one part and another part in another, and this is what is meant by division: the division of the inherence according to the division of the subject. And an objection was raised against this reasoning: that it is permissible for the inherence to subsist in the totality of the divided subject as a totality, and be a predicate for it without being divided by its division; such is called non-saryani indwelling. So he [the author] pointed to it and to its answer by saying: 'And the statement of one who says: this which you have mentioned is valid only when the indwelling in the divided subject is saryani indwelling, since without it the division of the inherence by the division of its subject does not follow' — and it has no benefit, for we have proven that every part of the divided subject in which a predicate inheres is characterized by a part of it, and there is no meaning of saryani except that. But there is discussion [in this].
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof used by the mutakallimun to show that unity cannot exist externally. The proof is based on the premise that if a unity (as an accident) inheres in a divisible subject (like a body), the unity must itself become divisible. The argument runs through three cases: (1) if the unity is in one part only, then that part is the one, not the whole — which contradicts the assumption that the whole subject is one; (2) if the unity is not in any part, the subject cannot be described as one; (3) if it is in every part, either it is wholly in each part (making the one many) or it is distributed (so the unity divides). All three are impossible. The philosophers object by introducing the concept of 'non-saryani indwelling' (حلول غير سرياني), where the accident inheres in the subject as a whole without penetrating its parts. The author then gives the response: even in non-saryani indwelling, every part of the subject is still characterized by a part of the accident, leading to division. However, the commentator notes that there is debate about this response.

Key Terms
الواحد
القسمة
الحال
المحل
الحلول السرياني
الحلول غير السرياني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-saryani indwelling avoids the problem of division entirely, as the accident does not penetrate the parts.
Correction: The text argues that even in non-saryani indwelling, each part of the subject is characterized by a part of the accident, so division still occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the 'non-saryani indwelling' objection introduced by the philosophers?
A) The accident inheres in each part separately.
B) The accident inheres in the whole subject as a unit, not in the parts.
C) The accident does not inhere at all.
D) The accident divides into equal parts.

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that unity cannot inhere in a divisible subject without dividing, and the response to the non-saryani objection maintains that division is unavoidable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is One,' do you ever subconsciously imagine His oneness as something that can be broken into parts? What does this logical proof reveal about the contradiction of thinking of divine unity as inhering in a composite? How must your internal conception of tawhid change so that you never, even in your mind, attribute division to Allah?
?
Consider the philosophers' objection of 'non-saryani indwelling' — a comfortable middle ground. Is there an area in your faith where you try to hold onto a belief that seems logically comfortable but contradicts the rigorous implications of tawhid? What excuse do you make to avoid the full weight of Allah's absolute oneness?
?
The proof shows that unity cannot dwell in something divisible without dividing. Allah is absolutely one and indivisible. Is there any 'part' of your life where you treat Allah's unity as if it is separate from your daily actions? Where do you need to integrate your belief in tawhid so that your entire being reflects His oneness, leaving no part unaffected?

Proof that Unity is a Mental Consideration
Al-Jurjani proves unity is mental and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that unity is a mental consideration and how Al-Jurjani answers the objection that mental unity would be mentally divisible.

Core Idea
Unity cannot be a real attribute because it would then be divisible.

This passage presents one of the most subtle arguments in kalam: proving that unity is not an existent quality but a mental concept. Al-Jurjani first establishes a key principle about qualities in divisible substrates, then applies it to unity, and finally answers a clever objection. By the end, you'll see why theologians held that unity is merely a consideration of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the structure: a principle about divisible substrates, its application to unity, the conclusion, then an objection and answer.
Pay attention to the logical flow; this is a dense argument.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage states that if a quality inheres in a divisible substrate, it must be divisible accordingly. Understand this principle and why it is accepted.
This principle is crucial for the proof.
3
Follow the objection and answer
Notice the dilemma: whether unity is existent or mental, division seems necessary. See how Al-Jurjani escapes by invoking the intellect's holistic perspective.
The answer shows that mental considerations do not entail division in the substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : المتكلمون أنكروا العدد جزء من أجزاء المحل لم يكن صفة له ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة لجواز أن يكون حالا في المجموع من حيث هو ولا يكون حالاً في شيء من أجزائه كالنقطة الخط، والإضافة في محلها عند القائل بوجودهما هذا وإذا ثبت أن الحال في المحل المنقسم يجب أن يكون منقسما بحسبه ( فإذا كانت الوحدة وجودية لزم انقسامها ) بانقسام الجسم الذي حلت فيه ( وإنه ) أعني انقسام الوحدة (ضروري البطلان ) فوجب أن تكون الوحدة أمراً اعتبارياً فإن قلت: الوحدة التي هي صفة للجسم بحسب نفس الأمر، إن كانت وجودية وجب انقسامها بحسب الخارج، وإن كانت اعتبارية وجب انقسامها بحسب التوهم. وكلاهما محال قلت : إنَّ العقل يعتبر المجموع من حيث الإجمال فيعتبر له عدم الانقسام أعني الوحدة فلا يلزم انقسامها أصلاً لأن محلها ملحوظ من حيثية لا مجال فيها للانقسام ولا يمكن اعتبار الحيثيات العقلية في الأمور الخارجية
In the second observatory, the fifth objective: The theologians denied number. A part of the parts of the substrate is not an attribute for it, and the claim of necessity is not accepted because it is possible for it to be a state in the whole as such without being a state in any of its parts, like the point on the line, and the relation in its substrate according to the one who affirms their existence. And if it is established that the state in the divisible substrate must be divisible according to it (then if unity were existent, its division would be necessitated) by the division of the body in which it inheres (and that) i.e., the division of unity (is necessarily false). So it is necessary that unity be a mental consideration. Now if you say: Unity which is an attribute of the body according to the matter itself, if it were existent, its division externally would be necessitated, and if it were mental, its division mentally would be necessitated, and both are impossible. Say: The intellect considers the whole as a whole, so it attributes to it non-division, i.e., unity, so its division does not follow at all because its substrate is considered from a perspective in which there is no room for division, and it is not possible to consider mental aspects in external things.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a proof that unity (al-wahda) is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). The argument proceeds as follows: first, it is established that if a quality (حال) resides in a divisible substrate (محل منقسم), it must be divisible according to that substrate. Then, if unity were an existent quality, it would have to be divisible along with the body in which it inheres. But the division of unity is clearly impossible (بطلان ضروري). Therefore, unity cannot be existent; it must be a mental consideration. An objector raises a dilemma: if unity is existent, it would be divisible externally; if it is merely mental, it would be divisible mentally—both seem impossible. The author answers that the intellect considers the whole (المجموع) from the perspective of its totality (من حيث الإجمال), and thus attributes non-division (i.e., unity) to it. The substrate is considered under a aspect that admits no division, so no division follows. Moreover, mental considerations (الحيثيات العقلية) cannot be applied to external things, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الاعتبارية
المحل المنقسم
الانقسام
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity must be a real attribute because we perceive things as one.
Correction: The argument shows that if unity were real, it would be divisible, which is absurd. It is a mental concept applied to wholes.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why must unity (al-wahda) be a mental consideration (amr i'tibari)?
A) Because all qualities are mental.
B) Because if unity were existent, it would have to be divisible along with the body, which is impossible.
C) Because the body is not divisible.
D) Because the objector conceded.

Key Takeaway
Unity is not an existent quality; it is a mental consideration because if it were existent, it would have to be divisible, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am one person,' do you feel that oneness is a real property of your body, or is it how your mind grasps your inseparable existence? What difference does it make in how you view your own identity?
?
If unity is only a mental consideration, then why does your heart feel that your tawheed is about affirming a real, undivided oneness of Allah? Does this passage challenge you to think about how we speak of Allah's unity?
?
Consider your own tendency to think of things as 'one thing'—a family, a community, a goal. How does realizing that unity is a mental tool, not a real attribute, change how you value those concepts? Are they less real, or more profound?

Proof that Multiplicity is Non-Existent: Second Approach
Al-Jurjani presents the second approach proving that multiplicity is non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second approach to prove that multiplicity is non-existent, including the reductio ad absurdum of the two possible ways multiplicity could be existent.

Core Idea
Multiplicity cannot be a real attribute because any way of being existent leads to absurdity.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents an intricate proof that multiplicity (al-kathra) is not a real, existent attribute but merely a non-existent concept. The argument assumes the opposite and then examines all possible ways multiplicity could exist, showing each leads to contradiction. As you read, follow the logical steps carefully; this is a classic kalam argument by exhaustion.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the overall structure
The proof assumes multiplicity is existent and then rules out all possibilities by examining how it could subsist.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the first possibility
Multiplicity subsisting in the many as many (min haythu huwa kathir). This requires the individual (al-wahid al-shakhsi) to subsist in the many.
This possibility has two subcases.
3
Examine the two subcases
First subcase: the individual subsists wholly in each of the many. This is intuitively false and leads to absurdities like duality making each one two. Second subcase: subsistence by distribution, which would mean the attribute is not a single unified entity.
Both subcases are impossible.
4
Consider the second possibility
Multiplicity subsists through an accidental attribute that makes the many one. Then the discussion shifts to that attribute, leading to a similar dilemma.
The text cuts off, but the pattern suggests an infinite regress.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all options for multiplicity being existent are impossible, multiplicity must be non-existent.
The proof is complete in principle even though the text is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : المتكلمون أنكروا العدد ( وثانيهما ) أي ثاني المسلكين ( أن يدل ابتداء ) أي من غير استعانة بعدمية الوحدة ) على أن الكثرة عدمية (وإلا وإن لم تكن عدمية بل وجودية ( فإن قامت ) والأظهر أن يقال وإلا قامت ؛ أي الكثرة ( بالكثير) إذ لا يتصور قيامها بذاتها ولا بغير الكثير، وحينئذ ( فإما ) أن تقوم بالكثير ( من حيث هو كثير فيلزم قيام الواحد ( الشخصي ) بالكثير) فإن قام ذلك الواحد بتمامه بكل واحد من الكثير كان مما علم بطلانه بالبديهة مع استلزامه هاهنا محالاً آخر، فإن الاثنينية مثلاً، لو قامت بكل واحد من الواحدين كان الواحد اثنين وإن قام بالكثير على سبيل التوزيع بأن يقوم شيء من الاثنينية بهذا، وشيء آخر بذاك لم تكن الاثنينية صفة واحدة وحدة شخصية كما ادعيتموه ( أو ) تقوم بالكثير ) من حيث عرض له أمر صار به واحداً فننقل الكلام إليه أي إلى ذلك الأمر الذي صار به الكثير شيئاً واحداً صالحاً لأن يحل فيه واحد شخصي فنقول ذلك الأمر إما أن يحل في الكثير من حيث هو كثير وأنه
In the second observatory, the fifth objective: The theologians denied number. (And the second) i.e., the second of the two ways (is to prove initially) i.e., without seeking help from the non-existence of unity (that multiplicity is non-existent). (And if not, i.e., if it is not non-existent but existent) (then it subsists) and it is more apparent to say: and if it subsists (in the many) since it is not conceivable that it subsist by itself nor in other than the many. And then (either) it subsists in the many (as many, then the subsistence of the individual (i.e., the particular one) in the many is necessitated). If that individual subsists in its entirety in each one of the many, it is known to be false by intuition, and it also entails another absurdity here: for example, if duality subsisted in each of the two ones, then the one would be two; and if it subsists in the many by distribution, such that something of duality inheres in this and something else in that, then duality would not be a single attribute, a personal unity as you claim. (Or) it subsists in the many (because of an accidental attribute that made it one, then we transfer the discussion to that attribute, i.e., to that which made the many one thing suitable for a particular one to inhere in it. Then we say: that attribute either inheres in the many as many, and that
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof that multiplicity (al-kathra) is non-existent ('adamiyya). It is the second of two approaches. The proof begins by assuming the opposite: if multiplicity were existent (wujūdiyya), it must subsist (taqūmu) in the many (al-kathīr), because it cannot subsist by itself nor in something else. Then two possibilities are examined. First, it subsists in the many as many (min ḥaythu huwa kathīr). This would require that the individual (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣī) subsist in each of the many. If the individual subsists wholly in each, it is known by intuition to be false, and it leads to absurdities: for example, if duality subsisted in each of the two ones, then each one would be two. If the individual subsists by distribution (tawzī'), such that part of it is in one and part in another, then the attribute (like duality) would not be a single unified attribute, contradicting the claim. Second, it might subsist in the many through an accidental attribute that makes the many one. Then the discussion shifts to that attribute: it either inheres in the many as many, leading to a similar regress, or in some other way. The passage cuts off, but the argument aims to show that all options are impossible, so multiplicity cannot be existent.

Key Terms
الكثرة
العدمية
القيام
التوزيع
الواحد الشخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiplicity is a positive, existent attribute of things.
Correction: The proof shows that if multiplicity were existent, it would lead to absurdities. Therefore, it must be merely a non-existent, relative concept.

Checkpoint
In the second approach, what is the first possibility considered if multiplicity (al-kathra) were existent?
A) It subsists in the many as many.
B) It subsists in the many through a unifying attribute.
C) It subsists by itself.
D) It does not subsist at all.

Key Takeaway
The second approach proves that multiplicity cannot be a real existent attribute because all logical possibilities for its existence lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that multiplicity is not a real attribute; our perception of 'many' is relative and non-existent. Think about the many blessings you count daily—your health, wealth, relationships. If each of these is not a separate, real entity but a reflection of Allah's single, infinite generosity, how would your gratitude change? Will you train yourself to see the One behind the many?
?
Al-Jurjani uses pure reason to show that if multiplicity were real, it would lead to logical absurdities. Do you trust that your intellect can uncover such metaphysical truths about Allah's creation? Or do you shy away from using reason in 'Aqeedah? What is holding you back from digging deeper into these proofs today?
?
The proof shows that even the number two cannot be a real attribute without contradiction. If numbers—something so basic—are non-existent, what does that say about your attachment to counting your sins and good deeds? Are you relying on a numerical tally for your salvation, or on Allah's mercy? How will you shift your focus from quantity to quality in your worship from now on?

First Proof of Philosophers for Continuous Quantity
The author presents and defends the first proof from varying dimensions on a single body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof for the existence of continuous quantity (al-jism al-ta'līmī) based on the varying dimensions of a single body, including the objection and the distinction between potential and actual dimensions.

Core Idea
A single body's changing shapes proves that continuous quantity is real.

In this passage, the author presents the philosophers' first proof for the existence of continuous quantity (al-jism al-ta'līmī). By examining a single candle reshaped into different forms, they argue that the body's dimensions must be distinct quantities that exist in it. This lesson walks you through the proof, its defense against objections, and the key distinction between potential and actual dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist. Then read the translation. Notice how the author uses the example of a candle given different shapes.
Focus on the phrases 'تتوارد عليه مقادير مختلفة' and 'مع بقاء جسميته المخصوصة'.
2
Identify the key clarifications
The author clarifies two points: 1) 'dimensions' are meant in the linguistic sense (sensible dimensions), and 2) the persistence of specific corporeality assumes dimension is not a specification of corporeality. Note these defenses against potential objections.
These clarifications prevent circularity and begging the question.
3
Examine the objection
The objection states that only shape changes, not dimension, because the area (product) remains the same. Find this objection in the passage.
The objection is introduced by 'لا يقال'.
4
Understand the response
The author replies by distinguishing potential vs. actual dimensions. The area is one in potentiality, but in actuality the extension in each direction differs. This shows the dimensions actually change.
This distinction is crucial for the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء في إثباته بوجهين: الأول أن الجسم الواحد كالشمعة مثلاً تتوارد عليه مقادير مختلفة فتارة يجعل طوله شبراً وعرضه ذراعاً وتارة بالعكس وتارة مدوراً وتارة مكعباً وهو ما يحيط به سطوح ستة هي مربعات متساوية وحينئذ فقد توارد عليه مقادير مختلفة مع بقاء جسميته المخصوصة ما لم يطرأ عليه انفصال، وتلك المقادير المختلفة كميات سارية فيه ممتدة في الجهات الثلاث وهي الجسم التعليمي. قوله : ( مقادير) بالمعنى اللغوي أعني المقادير المحسوسة فلا يتوهم المصادرة . قوله : (مع بقاء جسميته المخصوصة) هذا إنما يتم لو لم تكن المقدار من مخصصات الجسمي، وهو ممنوع إلى أن يقوم الدليل عليه . قوله : ( تتوارد عليه مقادير مختلفة) المراد بالمقادير هاهنا هو المقادير المتعارفة التي ينكرها أحد وكذا المراد بالسطح فيما سيأتي فلا يرد أن فيه مصادرة لتوقفه على ثبوت المقادير. لا يقال : لا يتغير المقدار فيما ذكرتم من المثال بل يختلف الأشكال واختلافه لا يستلزم اختلاف المقدار ( إذ المساحة واحدة ) في جميع هذه الصور المتبدلة ( لأنا نقول المساحة واحدة بالقوة أي مضروب أحدهما كمضروب الآخر، وأما بالفعل فالاختلاف في المقدار (ظاهر) لأن ذلك الجسم له مع التدوير كمية مخصوصة في الجهات ومع التكعيب كمية أخرى ممتدة فيها على وجه آخر فالمقادير المتواردة مختلفة بالفعل وإن كانت متحدة بالقوة من حيث أن المساحة الحاصلة منها بطريق الضرب واحدة وهذا الاتحاد لا يقدح في إثبات ما هو المطلوب
And the philosophers argued for its existence by two proofs: The first is that one body, like a candle for example, receives different dimensions; sometimes its length is made a span and its width a cubit, and sometimes the opposite, and sometimes round, and sometimes cubic, which is what is surrounded by six surfaces that are equal squares. Then various dimensions befall it while its specific corporeality remains as long as no separation occurs to it. And those different dimensions are quantities permeating it extended in the three directions, and that is the teaching body (al-jism al-ta'līmī). His saying: (Dimensions) in the linguistic sense, I mean the sensible dimensions, so no circularity is imagined. His saying: (with the persistence of its specific corporeality) this is valid only if dimension is not among the specifications of corporeality, and that is denied until evidence is established for it. His saying: (various dimensions befall it) what is meant by dimensions here is the conventional dimensions that are denied by no one, and similarly what is meant by surface in what follows, so it is not objected that there is circularity because it depends on the existence of dimensions. It cannot be said: The dimension does not change in the example you mentioned; rather shapes differ, and their difference does not entail difference of dimension (since the area is one) in all these changing forms. (For we say: the area is one in potentiality, i.e., the product of one is like the product of the other. But in actuality, the difference in dimension is manifest, because that body has, with rounding, a specific quantity in the directions, and with cubing, another quantity extended in them in another way. So the occurring dimensions are different in actuality, even if they are one in potentiality in that the area resulting from them by multiplication is one. And this unity does not detract from proving what is sought.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from the philosophers for the existence of continuous quantity. The proof uses the example of a candle that can be shaped differently (rectangle, round, cube) while remaining the same corporeality. These different shapes have different dimensions, which must be distinct quantities inhering in the body, i.e., the 'teaching body' (al-jism al-ta'līmī) which is continuous quantity. The commentary clarifies that 'dimensions' here means conventional, sensible dimensions, not the philosophical term, to avoid circularity. An objection is raised that only shape changes, not the actual dimension, since the area (product) remains constant. The answer: area is the same in potentiality (i.e., the numerical product), but in actuality the extension in each direction differs, so dimensions differ in actuality. Thus the body undergoes real change in its continuous quantity, proving its existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Changing the shape of a body does not change its dimensions because the area (product) remains the same.
Correction: While the area (product) may be the same in potentiality, the actual extension in each direction differs, so dimensions differ in actuality.

Checkpoint
